<score>S</score>

So the minimum is at x = λ, but note that x must be in the allowed interval. The allowed interval is [0, sqrt(2-λ^2)]. So the minimum of f over the interval is either at the vertex if it lies within the interval, or at an endpoint. So we need to consider whether λ ≤ sqrt(2-λ^2). This is equivalent to λ^2 ≤ 2-λ^2 => 2λ^2 ≤ 2 => λ^2 ≤ 1 => λ ≤ 1. So for λ ≤ 1, the vertex is within the interval. Then the minimum value is f(λ) = 5λ^2 - 4λ*λ + 2λ^2 = 5λ^2 - 4λ^2 + 2λ^2 = 3λ^2. So the minimum Q_3 is 3λ^2. She wants this to be > 4, i.e., 3λ^2 > 4 => λ^2 > 4/3 => λ > 2/√3 ≈ 1.1547. But for λ > 1, the vertex x=λ might be outside the interval because sqrt(2-λ^2) becomes smaller. For λ > 1, we have sqrt(2-λ^2) is defined only if λ^2 ≤ 2, so λ ≤ √2 ≈1.414. For λ in (1, √2], the allowed x_2 is in [0, sqrt(2-λ^2)]. Since λ > 1, we have λ > sqrt(2-λ^2)? Then the function f(x) is decreasing on [0, sqrt(2-λ^2)] because the vertex is at x=λ > sqrt(2-λ^2), and the quadratic is convex, so it is decreasing up to the vertex. So the minimum on the interval is at the right endpoint x = sqrt(2-λ^2). So we need to check if f(sqrt(2-λ^2)) > 4. Compute f(s) = 5λ^2 - 4λ s + 2 s^2, with s = sqrt(2-λ^2). Then f(s) = 5λ^2 - 4λ s + 2(2-λ^2) = 5λ^2 - 4λ s + 4 - 2λ^2 = 3λ^2 - 4λ s + 4. So we need 3λ^2 - 4λ s + 4 > 4 => 3λ^2 - 4λ s > 0 => λ(3λ - 4s) > 0 => 3λ > 4s => 3λ > 4 sqrt(2-λ^2). Square both sides: 9λ^2 > 16(2-λ^2) => 9λ^2 > 32 - 16λ^2 => 25λ^2 > 32 => λ^2 > 32/25 = 1.28 => λ > sqrt(1.28) ≈ 1.131. So for λ > 1.131, the condition holds. For λ between 1 and 1.131, the minimum Q_3 might be ≤4, so Alice might not be able to force a win with this strategy. But maybe she can choose a different first move? Let's explore.

We need to determine for which λ Alice has a winning strategy. The game is infinite horizon, but we can think of the "critical" λ where the game becomes a draw. We have found that for λ > √2, Alice wins by always choosing λ. For λ = √2, she can win by choosing x_1=√2, x_3=2√2. For λ slightly less than √2, say λ=1.4, which is less than √2≈1.414? Actually, 1.4 < 1.414, so λ=1.4 is in (1.131, √2). According to the above, with x_1=λ, she can win if λ > 1.131. So for λ=1.4, she can win by that strategy. So it seems that for λ > 1.131, she can win. But what about λ between 0.8944 and 1.131? For example, λ=1.1. Then with x_1=1.1, we need to check if she can force a win. Let's compute: λ=1.1, λ^2=1.21. Then Bazza's allowed x_2: x_2^2 ≤ 2 - 1.21 = 0.79, so x_2 ≤ sqrt(0.79)≈0.889. Then Alice can choose x_3 = 2λ - x_2 = 2.2 - x_2. Then Q_3 = λ^2 + x_2^2 + (2λ - x_2)^2 = 1.21 + x_2^2 + (2.2 - x_2)^2 = 1.21 + x_2^2 + 4.84 - 4.4 x_2 + x_2^2 = 6.05 - 4.4 x_2 + 2 x_2^2. The minimum over x_2 in [0,0.889] occurs at x_2 = λ? But λ=1.1 > 0.889, so the function is decreasing on the interval. So the minimum is at the right endpoint x_2=0.889. Then Q_3_min = 6.05 - 4.4*0.889 + 2*0.79 = 6.05 - 3.9116 + 1.58 = 3.7184. That's less than 4. So she cannot guarantee Q_3 > 4. But maybe she can choose a different x_1? She could choose x_1 smaller to allow Bazza to choose a smaller x_2? Actually, she wants to maximize her chance. She could try to choose x_1 such that the minimum Q_3 is > 4. Let's analyze the general strategy: On her first turn, she chooses x_1 = a (≥0). Then Bazza chooses x_2 = b (subject to b^2 ≤ 2 - a^2). Then she chooses x_3 = c (subject to c ≤ 3λ - (a+b)). She wants to make Q_3 = a^2 + b^2 + c^2 > 4. She can choose c as large as possible, so c = 3λ - (a+b) (if that is nonnegative). Then Q_3 = a^2 + b^2 + (3λ - a - b)^2. She wants this to be > 4 for all allowed b. So she needs that even the minimum over b ∈ [0, sqrt(2-a^2)] of Q_3 is > 4. She can choose a to maximize this minimum. This is a kind of "maximin" problem.

Alternatively, she could adopt a strategy that forces a win on a later turn. Perhaps she can win by making Q_n exceed n at some even turn, not necessarily turn 4. She could try to make Q_5 > 5, etc. So we need to find the threshold λ where Alice can force a win.

We should analyze the game more generally. The game is a two-player game with alternating moves, and the constraints are linear for Alice and quadratic for Bazza. The game ends when a player cannot move. So we can think of the game as a "pursuit" where Alice tries to make the sum of squares exceed the turn number, and Bazza tries to keep it below. Since the constraints are cumulative, we can consider the "potential" of the game.

Let's denote the state after each turn. We can think of the game as a kind of "alternating constraints" game. A common technique is to consider the set of possible sequences that can be sustained indefinitely. If there is a strategy for one player to force a win, then the other player cannot force a draw. So we need to find the critical λ where the game becomes a draw.

From our experiments:
- λ > √2: Alice wins.
- λ = √2: Alice wins (by deviating from the "always λ" strategy).
- λ between something and √2: Alice might win? For λ=1.4, we found a strategy that wins on turn 4. For λ=1.1, we found that the strategy with x_1=λ gives Q_3_min ≈3.718 <4, so she cannot guarantee a win on turn 4. But maybe she can win on a later turn? For example, she could choose x_1=0, then Bazza chooses x_2=√2, then she chooses x_3=0, then Bazza chooses x_4=√2, etc. That leads to a draw. So she needs to find a strategy that forces Bazza to eventually exceed his sum-of-squares bound. Since Bazza can always choose zero, the only way to force him to lose is to make the sum of squares before his turn exceed the turn number. So she needs to make the cumulative sum of squares large enough. She can try to make a large number on her turn, but she must respect her sum constraint. So the question is: For a given λ, can she choose a sequence of numbers on her turns such that the cumulative sum of squares eventually exceeds the turn number, while the cumulative sum is bounded by λ n? Since she only controls her numbers, and Bazza's numbers can be chosen to minimize her advantage, she must consider that Bazza will choose numbers that keep the sum of squares low (by choosing zero) and also increase the sum to reduce her budget. So the worst case for her is that Bazza always chooses zero. Then the game reduces to: Alice chooses x_1, x_3, x_5, ... such that for each odd n, S_n ≤ λ n, and she wants to make Q_n > n for some odd n (since after her move, it's Bazza's turn at the next even turn, and if Q_n > n, then Bazza loses). But note that if she makes Q_n > n at an odd n, then Bazza loses on his next turn. So she wants to achieve Q_{2k-1} > 2k-1? Actually, the turn number is n, and she wants Q_n > n. Since she moves on odd n, she can try to make Q_{2k-1} > 2k-1. But wait, the condition for Bazza to lose on his turn at n=2k is Q_{2k-1} > 2k. So she needs Q_{2k-1} > 2k, not just >2k-1. Because the constraint for Bazza is Q_n ≤ n, and his turn is at n=2k. So if Q_{2k-1} > 2k, then even with x_{2k}=0, Q_{2k}=Q_{2k-1}>2k, so he cannot move. So she needs Q_{2k-1} > 2k. So her goal is to make the sum of squares of her numbers (since Bazza chooses zero) exceed the next even turn number.

Thus, assuming Bazza always chooses zero, the game is: Alice chooses nonnegative numbers x_1, x_3, x_5, ... such that for each odd n=2k-1, the sum S_{2k-1} = sum_{i=1}^{k} x_{2i-1} ≤ λ (2k-1). She wants to make Q_{2k-1} = sum_{i=1}^{k} x_{2i-1}^2 > 2k for some k. She can choose her numbers adaptively. So we need to find the set of λ for which there exists a strategy for Alice to achieve Q_{2k-1} > 2k for some k, regardless of Bazza's moves? But if Bazza always chooses zero, then her strategy is just a sequence of numbers. She can choose them to maximize the sum of squares given the sum constraint. So the question reduces to: For a given λ, can she find a sequence of nonnegative numbers (x_1, x_3, ...) such that for all k, sum_{i=1}^{k} x_{2i-1} ≤ λ (2k-1), and for some k, sum_{i=1}^{k} x_{2i-1}^2 > 2k? This is a kind of "budget" problem: She has a linear budget for the sum, and she wants to achieve a quadratic sum. Since the constraints are at each step, she can choose the numbers to maximize the sum of squares given the cumulative sum budget. This is similar to the problem of maximizing sum of squares given a sum constraint, but with the additional constraint that the sum at each step must not exceed λ times the step number. Since she can choose her numbers adaptively, she can always choose the maximum possible at each step given the previous sum. So the maximum possible sum of squares after k steps is achieved by making the numbers as large as possible, but respecting the cumulative sum constraint. This is like: She wants to maximize Q_k = sum x_i^2 subject to sum x_i ≤ λ (2k-1) (since she has k steps) and also each intermediate sum constraint? Alice's budget for x_7: 7λ - S_6 = 5.6 - 5.567 = 0.033. She chooses x_7=0.033. Then Q_7 = 2.307 + 0.001 = 2.308. Then Bazza at turn 8: Q_7=2.308, he can choose x_8 up to sqrt(8-2.308)=sqrt(5.692)=2.386. So it seems the game can continue. But we need to see if Bazza can eventually make Alice unable to move. He wants to make S_n > λ n at some odd n. Since he can choose numbers, he can try to push the sum up. But his numbers are limited by the sum-of-squares constraint. The maximum sum he can impose over k even turns is k√2. So after his turn at 2k, S_{2k} ≤ k√2. For λ=0.8, we need to see if there is a k such that k√2 > 0.8*(2k+1). This is k*1.414 > 1.6k + 0.8 => 0.414k > 0.8 => k > 0.8/0.414 ≈1.93, so for k≥2, we have 2*1.414=2.828 > 0.8*5=4? Wait, 0.8*5=4, so 2.828 < 4, so not. For k=3, 3*1.414=4.242, 0.8*7=5.6, still less. For k=10, 14.14, 0.8*21=16.8, still less. Actually, we need k√2 > 2λ k + λ? That's k(√2-2λ) > λ. For λ=0.8, √2-1.6=0.814, so 0.814k > 0.8 => k > 0.8/0.814 ≈0.983, so for k≥1, we have k√2 > 1.6k + 0.8? Let's check k=1: 1.414 > 1.6+0.8=2.4? No. So the inequality is not satisfied for small k. Actually, the condition for Bazza to win is that he can force S_{2k} > λ(2k+1) for some k. Since he can choose his numbers to maximize sum, the maximum S_{2k} is k√2. So he can win if there exists k such that k√2 > λ(2k+1). This is equivalent to k(√2-2λ) > λ. For λ < √2/2, the left side is positive for large k, and the inequality will hold for sufficiently large k. For λ=0.8, √2/2=0.707, so λ=0.8 > 0.707, so the left side is negative? Actually, √2-2λ = 1.414-1.6 = -0.186, so k(√2-2λ) is negative, so the inequality k(√2-2λ) > λ is impossible. So Bazza cannot win by that strategy. So for λ > √2/2, Bazza cannot win. So the only way for Bazza to win is if λ < √2/2. For λ between √2/2 and 2√2/3, we have that Bazza cannot win, and Alice can win? But we just saw that for λ=0.8, which is >0.707, Alice's strategy with x_1=0 gives c=3*0.8-√2=2.4-1.414=0.986, which is less than √2, so Q_3=2+0.972=2.972<4, so she cannot win on turn 4. But maybe she can win on a later turn? Let's analyze the game more generally for λ between √2/2 and 2√2/3. We need to see if Alice can force a win by a different strategy, perhaps by not setting x_1=0, but by setting x_1 positive to allow her to make a larger x_3? Actually, we already computed that the best she can do on turn 4 is to set a=0, giving Q_3 = 2 + (3λ - √2)^2. For λ=0.8, this is 2 + (2.4-1.414)^2 = 2 + (0.986)^2 = 2+0.972=2.972<4. So she cannot win on turn 4. Could she win on turn 6? She could try to make a larger number on turn 5? Let's think about the game as a "budget" problem. She has a linear budget for the sum, and she wants to make the sum of squares exceed the turn number at some odd turn. Since Bazza can always choose zero, the worst case for her is that Bazza chooses zero. Then the game is: She chooses x_1, x_3, x_5, ... such that S_{2k-1} ≤ λ (2k-1). She wants to make Q_{2k-1} > 2k for some k. This is a classic problem: Given a linear budget, what is the maximum possible sum of squares? She can concentrate all her budget into one number, but she must respect the intermediate constraints. For example, she could set x_1 = λ, then she has used her budget for turn 1. For turn 3, she can set x_3 = 3λ - λ = 2λ, but then S_3 = λ+2λ=3λ, which is exactly the budget. Then Q_2 = λ^2 + (2λ)^2 = 5λ^2. For λ=0.8, this is 5*0.64=3.2, which is less than 4. So she cannot win on turn 4. But she could set x_1 = 0, then she has a larger budget for later. With x_1=0, she can set x_3 = 3λ, then S_3=3λ, Q_2=0+9λ^2=9λ^2. For λ=0.8, 9*0.64=5.76>4, so she can win on turn 4? Wait, but she must also respect Bazza's constraint. If she sets x_1=0, then Bazza can choose x_2=√2, which is allowed because Q_1=0, so his constraint is Q_2 ≤2, and he can choose x_2=√2. Then S_2 = 0+√2=1.414. Then her budget for x_3 is 3λ - (0+√2)=2.4-1.414=0.986, so she cannot choose x_3=3λ. So the worst case is that Bazza chooses a positive number to reduce her budget. So she cannot assume Bazza chooses zero; she must consider that Bazza will choose numbers to minimize her ability to win. So the worst-case scenario for her is that Bazza chooses numbers that maximize the sum of his numbers given his sum-of-squares budget. So we need to analyze the game where Bazza chooses his numbers optimally to prevent Alice from winning, and Alice chooses her numbers optimally to try to win.

Thus, we need to find the set of λ for which Alice has a strategy to force a win, i.e., she can guarantee that eventually Bazza cannot move. This is a two-player game with perfect information. We can try to find a "strategy stealing" or "invariant" that determines the winner.

Let's denote the state after each turn. The game is infinite horizon, but we can think of the "critical" λ where the game is a draw. Often, in such games, the threshold is where the "optimal" sequence that keeps the game going forever is balanced. We have found that for λ > 2√2/3, Alice can win on turn 4 by setting x_1=0. But is that always a winning strategy? She sets x_1=0. Then Bazza chooses x_2. He will choose x_2 to minimize her chance. Since he wants to avoid losing, he will choose x_2 as large as possible, i.e., x_2 = sqrt(2 - 0^2) = √2. Then S_2 = √2. Then she chooses x_3 = 3λ - √2 (since she wants to make Q_3 as large as possible; she can choose x_3 up to 3λ - S_2). She chooses x_3 = 3λ - √2. Then Q_3 = 0 + 2 + (3λ - √2)^2. She wins if this > 4, i.e., (3λ - √2)^2 > 2, i.e., 3λ - √2 > √2, i.e., λ > 2√2/3. So for λ > 2√2/3, this strategy wins. For λ = 2√2/3, we have equality: Q_3 = 2 + (2√2 - √2)^2 = 2 + (√2)^2 = 4. Then Bazza can choose x_4=0, and the game continues. So Alice does not win on turn 4. But maybe she can win on a later turn? Let's analyze the game for λ = 2√2/3. We need to see if Alice can force a win by a different strategy. Perhaps she can set x_1 = something else to allow her to make a larger x_3? But we already saw that the maximum of (3λ - a - sqrt(2-a^2)) over a in [0, λ] is at a=0, giving 3λ - √2. For λ=2√2/3, this is √2. So the best she can do on turn 4 is to have Q_3 = 2 + (√2)^2 = 4. So she cannot win on turn 4. Could she win on turn 6? She could try to make a larger number on turn 5, but she must respect her sum constraint at turn 5. Let's consider a more general strategy: She could set x_1 = a, then Bazza chooses b = sqrt(2-a^2) (worst case). Then she chooses x_3 = c = 3λ - a - b. Then Q_3 = a^2 + b^2 + c^2 = 2 + c^2. If c^2 ≤ 2, she cannot win on turn 4. But she could then continue. At turn 5, Bazza chooses x_4 = d (subject to Q_4 ≤ 4). He will choose d to minimize her future chances. She then chooses x_5 = e (subject to S_5 ≤ 5λ). She wants to eventually make Q_n > n. This is like a "maximin" problem.

We need to find the critical λ where the game is a draw. From our experiments, it seems that for λ > 2√2/3, Alice wins. For λ = 2√2/3, we need to determine if she can still win. We need to determine if there is a strategy for her that guarantees that eventually the sum of squares before Bazza's turn exceeds the turn number.

We can think of the game as a "two-player" game where the "potential" is something like the "gap" between the sum of squares and the turn number. This is reminiscent of a game where players have linear and quadratic constraints, and the threshold is given by the "critical" λ where the game is a draw.
